preface.txt
O you, my very dear sons, I have completed for you this book which is the last of the science of medicine along with its completion, and have made the end through its expositions and its declarations. And it came to me in a vision that I should complete it for you with this treatise, which is part of the work of hands, that is to say "surgery".	AFTER finishing for you, my sons, this book which is the part of knowledge dealing with medicine in its entirety; and having made it as clear and explicit as possible, I thought it well to complete it for you by adding this treatise which concerns surgical operating.	0.79031384
And because the work of hands is neglected in our region and is altogether restricted, so the science is lost and diminished, and nothing remains of it except small descriptions in the books of the ancients that hands have copied. And from this has come error and vanity, and understanding is obscured, and knowledge is distanced.	For the skilled practitioner of operative surgery is totally lacking in our land and time; so that the knowledge of it is on the point of being blotted out and its remains lost; and there is nothing left of it except a few traces in the books of the Ancients; where, however, it has been so corrupted by the hands of scribes, and subjected to error and confusion, that its meaning has become obscured and its value diminished.	0.80621797
Now it seems to me that I want to revive it along with the organization of this treatise according to the way of exposition and declaration and abbreviation; and I will include along with it the forms of cautery irons, and other instruments of the work with the addition of the explanation and preparation of them, as is necessary.	Therefore I decided to revive this art by expounding, elucidating, and epitomizing it in this treatise; and to present the forms of the cauterizing irons and other operative instruments, since this is an adjunct to explanation and a vital necessity.	0.79478824
And because no good master of hands is found in our time, the art of medicine is diminished. And whoever undertakes it must excel in the science of anatomy, as Galen said, so much that he knows the arrangement of the limbs, and their forms and connections and separations, and has knowledge of the bones and nerves and arms and their number, and the course of the moving and resting veins, and the location of their origin.	Now this is the reason why there is no skilful operator in our day: the art of medicine is long and it is necessary for its exponent, before he exercises it, to be trained in anatomy as Galen has described it, so that he may be fully acquainted with the uses, forms, and temperament of the limbs; also how they are jointed, and how they may be separated; that he should understand fully also the bones, tendons, and muscles, their numbers and their attachments; and also the bloodvessels, both arteries and veins, with their relations.	0.8456429
And therefore Hippocrates said that the name with the name is much, and with the work is little; and especially in the art of the hand.	And so Hippocrates said: 'Though many are doctors in name, few are in reality, particularly on the surgical side.'	0.6812434
We tell you in the introduction of this book that whoever is not knowledgeable about the aforementioned things, let him beware that he does not fall into error and kill people;	We have already spoken of this in the introduction to this book. For he who is not skilled in as much anatomy as we have mentioned is bound to fall into error that is destructive of life.	0.5980303
thus I have often seen several of those who undertake this science and so abandon themselves without knowledge and without experience.	Thus I have seen many laying claim to this knowledge and boasting of it, but having neither knowledge nor experience.	0.7862043
For I saw a foolish physician who cut an abscess of scrofula in the neck of a woman, and cut the arteries of the neck; whereby so much blood came out that the woman fell dead in his hands.	I saw an ignorant doctor incise a scrofulous tumour in a woman's neck; and he cut certain arteries in the neck so that the woman bled until she fell dead before him.	0.8761073
And I saw another physician who removed a stone from an elderly man, and the stone was large; he also removed with it a piece of the bladder, and the man was dead within three days.	And I saw another doctor presume to take out a stone from a man of advanced age. The stone was large; he rushed blindly at the task and extracted the stone and with it a piece of the bladder itself; and the man was dead in about three days.	0.9235586
And I had already been called to remove it, and I saw the condition of the patient and the size of the stone, and I knew well that if he were cut, he would die from it.	I had myself been previously called in to extract the stone but from what I saw of the size of it and the condition of the patient I judged that the outcome would be so.	0.6619222
And I saw another physician who took into his care a duke of our land who had a broken leg, and along with the break there was a wound;	I saw another doctor who had a regular salary from one of the high officers of our country for medical treatment.	0.5832917
and the physician, full of ignorance, tightened the break over the wound with splints, binding it very tightly, and did not leave any opening for the wound to drain, and left him like that for a period of time;	There had occurred to a negro boy of his a fracture of the leg near the heel, together with a wound; the doctor rushed in, in his ignorance, and bound up the fracture, over the wound, very tightly, with pads and splints, not allowing the wound to breathe.	0.6862581
and ordered him not to untie it at all;	He then let him go as he wished and then dismissed him for some days bidding him not to loose the bandage.	0.44239348
and it remained like that until the leg was completely swollen, and the foot as well, and it was ruined.	Eventually his leg and foot swelled and he was close to death.	0.47619992
I was called to come to him, and I hastened to come and untie the leg; and when I had untied it, it was at peace and he felt no pain.	I was called in and made haste to loose the bandage, upon which some ease and reduction of the pain followed.	0.62542087
But nevertheless the infection was already established in the limb, and I could not heal it;	Nevertheless, gangrene had already taken hold on the limb and I could not hinder its progress; it.	0.7001161
and the infection did not cease to spread through the limb until the limb was completely lost.	continued to extend in the limb until he perished.	0.55217654
And I saw another physician who lanced a festering abscess, and then it inflamed due to the poor state of the patient's health.	And I saw another doctor incise a malignant tumour; after some days the place broke out in open ulceration, thereby adding to the misery of the patient.	0.79119927
For a canker, when it is full of melancholic humor, should not be touched with iron unless it is in such a place that all that is corrupted can be removed.	For a cancer that arises from a melancholic humour should never be touched by the knife unless it should happen to be in a part of the body which may be removed thoroughly and in its entirety.	0.6729127
And therefore, dearest son, you need to know that manual work is divided into two parts: namely, work where there is danger in several ways;	Wherefore you must know, my sons, that operative surgery is divided into two parts, namely, where operation is associated with the health of the patient, and, on the other hand, where it is for the most part fraught with danger.	0.65113044
and I will show in each place of this book where there is fear and danger.	Throughout the following book I point out wherever I have described an operation that is accompanied by fear and danger.	0.6975992
Therefore it is necessary that you protect yourself, so that foolish onlookers cannot speak ill of you.	It behoves you to be wary and to shun such, lest the ignorant have an opportunity to reproach and slander you.	0.58266765
So take up your tools wisely, and treat your patients gently and firmly, and use the best method so that by the grace of God you will succeed and be praised;	Show, then, caution and care for yourselves and gentleness and perseverance for your patients. Take the best road that leads to health and a happy outcome.	0.60998714
and leave the frightening diseases of severe nature;	Illnesses that are very threatening or difficult to cure, leave alone.	0.48345205
and remove yourself from that which makes you doubt, and have faith in yourself so that you can go from this world to the next in glory.	Purify yourselves of anything which you fear may cause doubt as to your religious life and your secular life; for this will perpetuate your good name and will elevate your fortunes in this world and the next.	0.5984757
Galen says in some of his admonishments: Do not undertake to treat a serious illness, lest you be called a foolish physician.	For Galen once said in certain of his admonitions: 'Do not treat mischievous maladies lest you get the name of mischievous doctors.'	0.8383776
I have divided this treatise into three chapters.	I have divided this treatise into three books:	0.9002188
The first chapter is about fire cauteries and sharp medicinal cauteries, and the chapter is organized from the head to the feet;	BOOK ONE. Concerning the actual cautery; and cauterization by caustics. It is divided into chapters set in order from the head to the feet.	0.66192013
and about the shapes of the instruments, and the cautery irons, and everything that is necessary for the work.	The shapes of the instruments are shown and the iron cauteries and all things necessary for operating.	0.7289436
The second chapter is about cutting and piercing, and cupping glasses, and wounds, and extracting arrows, and similar things;	BOOK TWO. Concerning incision and perforation; blood-letting and cupping; wounds and the extraction of arrows and the like.	0.75034493
and the chapter is organized with the shapes of the instruments.	The whole is divided into chapters in order; and illustrations of instruments are given.	0.6283297
The third chapter is about restoring dislocations and sprains, and similar things;	BOOK THREE. Concerning the setting of bones; dislocations; the treatment of sprains and the like.	0.60381246
and it is organized from the head down to the feet, and the shapes of the instruments.	This is similarly divided into chapters set in order from head to feet, with illustrations of instruments.	0.62387705

chapter-1.txt
Before we recall the work, it is necessary that I describe the quality of help and the harm of each complexion.	Before we expatiate on the actual operation of cautery it is necessary to mention its advantages and disadvantages and the temperament upon which it is to be used.	0.5493877
I say that the qualities of help, and of harm, are the longest discourse and subtle science and the hidden secret;	I say that a discourse on the advantage and disadvantage of cautery is a long one, a subtle learning, a hidden secret.	0.7390268
and many wise men spoke of this, and agreed on it;	A great number of wise men have spoken on the subject with differing opinions.	0.61632085
and their words suffice little for me, for I fear lengthiness.	Lest I should be over long I have reduced their opinions to a summary.	0.46071336
I say that cautery universally helps every complexion where there is matter, except for two complexions: that is to say, a hot complexion without matter, and a dry complexion without matter.	Now I say that the cautery has universal application for every ill constitution, whether organic or functional, with the exception of two: the functional hot temperament and the functional dry temperament.	0.65788
In a complexion both hot and dry with matter, some debated and said that cautery is a great help there; and others said the opposite: that cautery is not good for an illness that comes from heat and dryness. For the nature of fire is hot, and a hot illness should not be treated with heat, nor with dryness;	About the organic dry temperament the Ancients differed, some saying that cautery helps in a constitution of this kind, while others said the opposite: that cautery is of no use in a disease caused by heat or dryness; for fire is naturally hot and dry and it is obviously absurd to treat a hot dry sickness with a hot dry remedy.	0.8617166
and he who said the opposite claimed that cautery is a great help for a hot and dry illness that occurs in the bodies of men. For when you compare the body of a man and his moisture to the complexion of fire, you find the body cold.	But he who holds the opposite view says that actual cautery may help in the case of such hot dry illnesses as arise in the human body; for when you compare the human body, humid as it is, with the nature of fire you find the human body cold.	0.81672955
And I agree with this statement. For I have sometimes found it through experience.	I myself from much experience agree with this last opinion.	0.6385991
And in any case, no one should interfere except those who have experience, and who know through long practice the chapter on cauteries, and the diversity of human complexions, and the dispositions and causes of their illnesses, and the occurrences and the length of time;	However, no one should attempt this operation unless he has had long training and practice in the use of the cautery, and is fully acquainted with the various human temperaments, and the character of the complaints in themselves; their causes, symptoms, and duration.	0.7894381
We have no fear of other complexions, especially in cold and moist diseases. And all the physicians assembled and agreed on the cautery.	Concerning the rest of the temperaments there is no reason for fear, particularly the cold and humid distempers; all medical men are agreed and none differs concerning the utility of the cautery in such cases.	0.70964235
And know, son, that cautery with fire is among the secrets of healing, and of greater benefit than cautery with burning medicine.	Now you should know, my sons, that the actual cautery and its superiority over cauterization with chemical caustics is one of the secrets of medicine.	0.69262516
For fire is simple, and does not go beyond the limb that is cauterized, and does not harm other limbs except a little.	For fire is a simple substance having no action except upon the actual part cauterized; nor does it do more than slightly harm any adjacent part.	0.78194
Burning medicines spread from limb to limb, and cause diseases in the limb that are difficult to heal; and perhaps kill the patient.	But the effect of cauterization with caustic may spread to parts at a distance from that burnt; and also in the part cauterized it may give rise to a disease difficult to cure or even fatal.	0.62759465
Fire, because of its loftiness and the goodness of its substance, does not do this, unless there is excess.	Whereas fire, on the other hand, on account of the nobility of its nature and its superiority has no such effect unless it is overdone.	0.7949076
And this I have found for you through experiment and the length of service and study that I have put into the art, and it was based on the truth of things;	That has become clear to us by experience through length of service, devotion to the art, and acquaintance with the facts of the matter. So I need not prolong this discourse.	0.63832086
and for this I apologize for the length of the speech, for I do not want to deprive you of the secret that is contained in fire, and the quality of the work, how it expels diseases from bodies, as the philosopher says in demonstrating it; therefore receive it in your thoughts.	So I need not prolong this discourse. Were it not inappropriate to this my book I should relate to you a deep secret concerning fire, the manner of its action on bodies and its expulsion of diseases; with a reasoned philosophical discourse too subtle for your understanding.	0.69470274
And know, son, that they agreed on what time cauterizations should be done, and they chose spring.	Know also, my sons, that the Ancients disagreed also as to the fit time for cautery, affirming that spring was the best.	0.6595832
And I say that cautery is suitable in all seasons, because the sudden harm is lessened by the help of the cautery; and especially if the cautery is for necessary, sudden, and urgent pains that cannot wait for the time when greater harm might come to the limb.	Myself, I say that the cautery is suitable at all times; for whatever harm may arise from the season of the operation is utterly outdone by the benefit deriving from the cauterization itself; especially if the cautery is applied to pains that are severe, grievous, and swift, brooking no delay, because of the fear that the consequences may be more grave than the slight harm from the season.	0.8484975
And do not ever think, dear son, as the foolish physicians do; for the illness that is healed by cautery does not return; and they say it thus;	Nor let enter your minds, my sons, the groundless notion of the laity and of ignorant doctors, that the complaint cured by the cautery never comes back later; do not cleave to this opinion.	0.75180036
and they say it thus; but it is not as they think, because cautery is only a medicine that changes the complexion, and dries the humors that are the cause of the pain, and is better than medicine because of the swiftness of its effect, and because of the power of its work and its mastery.	The fact is not as they think; for the cautery is homologous to the drug which alters the constitution and dries up the humidities that give rise to disorders. But the cautery excels the drug by the rapidity of its success, the strength of its action, and the potency of its powers.	0.80550313
And it is possible that the illness sometimes returns according to the complexion of the patient and the location of the illness and its nature;	It may be that the disease will return at some time according to the temperament of the patient, and the persistence and virulence of the disease; and from the formation of a mass of superfluities in the body; from his neglect of himself in acquiring them from food; and from causes of a similar nature.	0.65442646
and the superfluities are gathered in him by the intake of foods and similar things, unless cautery is administered in a place where there are few superfluities and moisture, such as cautery of teeth for pain, and similar things, and such pain does not return; but this happens rarely.	Of course, if the disease to which the cautery is applied be a slight one, and in a part only slightly liable to superfluities and humidities, as, for example, the cauterization of a molar tooth for toothache, and the like, then it may happen that the pain does not return; but that happens rarely.	0.7010939
And many people do not understand why cautery is called the last resort of medicine, and it is true;	Another common saying is that the cautery is the final remedy; which is true, but not in the way which they mean.	0.5687205
but they do not believe that cautery is effective after medicine, but their understanding is false, and cautery is the last resort of medicine because when we administer methods of treatment for any disease and they are ineffective, then we administer cautery, and it is effective.	For they believe that after cauterization no treatment, either with or without medicine, will help.	0.5980098
Then it is clear that cautery is the last resort of medicine, not according to the understanding of foolish physicians.	It means something precisely the opposite: after various treatments have been applied to a certain disorder without success, then last of all we apply cauterization and this proves effective; whence comes about the saying that the cautery is the final remedyâ€”not in that sense in which it is popularly held by the laity and by many ignorant medical men.	0.4509822
The ancients said that cautery with a gold instrument is gentler than with iron, and they only said this because of the quality of gold and the nobility of its substance;	The Ancients have stated that cauterization by gold is more effective than by iron. Now they said that on account of the temperateness of gold and its noble character.	0.8133538
and they said that the site of the cautery does not suppurate, but this is not true because I have tested it, and I have found that it is true in some people;	They also stated that the site of cauterization does not become purulent, but that is not absolutely so; for I myself have experience of that and have found that it happens in some cases and not in other cases.	0.71060216
and gold cautery is better than iron, as they said. And yet when you heat a gold instrument, you do not know when it is hot because of the color of the gold, and it cools down quickly;	Cauterization by gold is indeed better and more successful than with iron, as they have stated; except that when you are heating the gold cautery in the fire you are uncertain, by reason of the redness of the gold, when it reaches the desired temperature.	0.7896185
and if it is heated too much; thus they make in the fire; and thus there is enough difficulty.	In addition to this it cools very quickly; and if you overheat it it melts in the fire, melts and runs, and the practitioner finds himself in difficulties.	0.60673016
Now I have organized this chapter from head to toe so that the way is easier to find.	Now I have arranged this book on cauterization by chapters, set in order from the head to the foot, to make it easy for the seeker to find what he wants.	0.74624413

This cautery is good for moisture and coldness on the brain, and there are two that are made for a disease called "soda," and for many humors that descend from the head to the eyes, and to the ears, and for excessive dreams and toothache and sore throat, and especially for all illnesses that come from coldness like paralysis and lethargy and apoplexy and similar illnesses.	This cauterization helps against excessive humidity and coldness on the brain, which cause headache, abundance of defluxions from the head to the regions of the eyes and ears, excess of sleep, toothache, sore throat, and in general any sort of disorder arising from frigidity, such as palsy, fits, apoplexy, and diseases similar to these.	0.70495075
The method for this cautery is that the patient be purged by a laxative medicine cleansing the head for three or four nights, depending on what the strength and age and constitution of the patient can tolerate; and then shave the patient's head.	The manner of performing this operation is first to bid the patient open the bowels with an evacuant which will also clear his head, for three or four nights, according to the strength, age, and habits of the patient.	0.832549

of the cautery of the head When in a part of the head comes a sudden pain, and the ailment extends to the eye, and the head is purged by cleansing medicines, and one has done the other treatments that I have described in the section on illnesses, and all this is of no use: then make cauteries in two ways, that is to say, with a sharp medicine and with iron.	When there occurs pain with headache in one side of the head and the pain extends to the eye; and the patient has cleared his head with purging drugs and there has been applied the other treatment that I have mentioned in the sections on diseases, but to no avail; in this disorder cauterization is of two sorts, either with caustic or with the actual cautery.	0.834157
The cautery with sharp medicine is that you take a clove of garlic, and remove the skin and cut off a little at both ends, and then split the temple where the pain is located in such a way that you can place the clove of garlic under the skin;	This is the manner of cauterization with caustic: take one clove of garlic; peel it and cut both ends off; then cut open the site of the pain in the temple with a broad scalpel till there is room to contain the clove under the skin; then introduce it under the skin till it lies completely hidden.	0.78471637
and when it is placed, bind it and hold it firmly and leave it thus for twelve hours; and then unbind it and take out the clove of garlic, and leave the wound like that for two or three days; then put on cotton dipped in butter until pus comes out.	Then bind up the wound tightly over it with pads and leave for fifteen hours; then unbind it, remove the garlic, and leave the wound open for two or three days; then apply cotton wool soaked in butter till it suppurates.	0.85741055
Afterwards treat it with ointment until it is healed;	Then dress with ointment till it heals.	0.78662467
and if you wish, do this with some burning medicines that I have written about in the twelfth treatise on burning medicines.	If you would rather, you may carry it out with one of the caustic drugs that I have included in the eighteenth treatise, on caustics.	0.77474856

And it is called "closed" because the head is shaped like a nail or has a slight curve, and in the middle has a small point;	It is called the claviform; the head is nailshaped in that there is a slight curvature with a small protuberance in the middle.	0.6026819
and place it on the painful spot, and hold your hand steady and turn the iron slightly, so that half the thickness of the skin is burned;	Apply it then to the site of the pain, hold your hand steady and revolve it little by little.	0.60650694
and lift your hand so that the artery, which is outside, is not burned, and a lot of blood flows out.	Let the thickness of skin burnt be about half; then remove your hand so as not to burn a subjacent artery, for thus a haemorrhage arises.	0.58447635

of a fistula wherever it may be Know that any wound or abscess, when it becomes chronic, is universally called a "fistula", and especially when it is deep and produces pus.	You should know that any wound or tumour, when it becomes old and chronic, and turns into an ulcer, and does not heal over, but discharges pus chronically and constantly, is generally called a fistula, in whatever part of the body it may be; but we call it a defluxion.	0.75914836
And a fistula, in truth, is a false, hard, white knot, where there is no feeling of pain, and is as deep as the feather of a bird;	The fistula proper is a compact hard white painless induration with a central hollow like a bird's quill; on which account some call it a quill.	0.64180505
and sometimes it discharges clear pus, and other times thick; sometimes little, and other times a lot;	At certain times it may be moist, constantly discharging pus; and again at certain times the moisture may cease; and the discharge may be greater or less; and it may be thick or thin.	0.76991796
and I have already spoken of it in the division of wounds, that any wound that is not healed, and where there is no flesh, is only due to one of nine causes: namely, because of lack of blood or bodily strength; or because of its malignancy; or because there is hard flesh inside, and on the edges, which prevents new flesh from growing;	I said previously in the appropriate section, when I was discussing abscesses, that any ulcer that would not heal nor show growth of tissue was due to one of nine causes. What cupping does is to draw blood out of the fine vessels dispersed over the flesh; for this reason it does not cause the strength to decline as does venesection; nor may you use cupping, either with fire or without fire, in any disease due to plethora, until the whole body has been evacuated.	0.8689455

There are many kinds of fractures, and various forms, and the causes are distinct;	The kinds of fracture occurring in the head are many, of various forms and differing causes.	0.7616799
for there are fractures caused by the blow of a sword, and the neck of the bone cuts the entire bone down to the syfac which is underneath the bone, as an axe does with wood; and this type of fracture is called a "hatchet fracture".	Some fractures are due to a sword-stroke which may cut right through the bone and reach the membrane beneath the bone, like an adze-cut in wood, for which reason this kind of cut is called 'ascial'.	0.7582461
Or the sword cuts the bone from above without piercing, and this type of fracture is called "complete";	Or the sword may cut through part of the bone, only slicing through the surface and not reaching the depth; this kind is called an absolute avulsion.	0.6472354
and the wound of these two fractures is large or small.	The wounds in these two fractures can be either big or small.	0.8331623
And if there is a blunt percussion, or a split or a cut from a stone, or from falling on a stone, or other similar things.	There are also some fractures accompanied by smashing or crushing; such as by a blow from a stone or by falling on a stone, and the like.	0.7290227
And if there is a piercing fracture, which reaches down to the membrane that is under the bone, or it is on the bone, and this wound is either large or small.	A fracture of this kind will be either penetrating, reaching the membrane beneath the bone; or superficial; the wound in these two fractures may be either big or small.	0.76349324
And also a wound which is placed in the bone as thin as a hair, and the crack is small, and therefore this fracture is called "hairline";	Then there is a type of fracture that is hidden in the bone, as fine as a hair, a thin crack; therefore this kind of fracture is called 'capillary'.	0.70955026
and if the wound, which is made either by striking with a stone, or by falling, or by a similar thing, causes the piece of bone to go down, and the place is sunken in as do the cauldron makers when they strike the cauldrons; then the stone enters inside.	There is a fracture due to a fall or a blow from a stone and the like, making a dent in the surface of the bone and a hollow at the site as occurs in a bronze bowl when a blow falls on it and a portion of it is pushed in.	0.76937425
And this happens more often to those who have moist bones in their head, like a child's head;	This mostly occurs in heads whose bones are soft, as those of children.	0.7589557
and it happens in all these types of fractures that small pieces are separated like splinters; and I will speak about it in its place.	Any of these kinds of fracture may also have splinters, jagged or otherwise; we shall deal with the treatment of all these in their place.	0.5916016
And we recognize all kinds of fractures by the depression above them, and probing with a tent, and by the corrupted flesh which is on top.	All these kinds of fracture are diagnosed by laying them bare and investigating them with probes and removing all the lacerated tissues over them.	0.5843234
The hairline fracture is recognized because when the bone is uncovered, and one rubs ink on it, the fracture appears black.	The 'capillary' kind is diagnosed by laying bare the bone, wiping it clean, and rubbing ink on it, when the fracture will appear black.	0.62976795
In the treatment of a fracture, pay attention to the patient's symptoms, and if you see any bad symptoms, such as vomiting bile, and loss of consciousness, and stretching, and swelling, and high fever, and the eyes bulging and red, and other similar symptoms with many variations, then it certainly cannot heal.	As to the treatment of the fracture: begin by paying attention to the patient's symptoms. If you observe any symptom clearly indicating something dangerous, such as bilious vomit, convulsion, mental derangement, loss of voice, fainting, high fever, protrusion and inflammation of the eyes, and similar symptoms, then leave the patient alone and do not treat him; for in the majority of cases these symptoms undoubtedly indicate the approach of death.	0.794888
And if you do not see alarming symptoms, and you hope for recovery, then begin treatment.	But if you find symptoms that do not alarm you and you see hope of recovery for him, then begin treatment as follows.	0.82984185
And when the wounded person first comes to you, and it is in winter, it is necessary to remove the bone before the fourteenth day as quickly as you can.	If he be brought to you immediately after his injury and it be the season of winter, you should try to remove the bone, at all events before the fourteenth day.	0.7781044
And if it is in summer, then hurry to remove the bone before the seventh day, before the tissue under the bone becomes corrupted, and the symptoms we have mentioned appear. And do not cut on one side, and the part is sharp on both sides, so that the cutting edge is towards the bone;	The lenticular part of it should be smooth and not for cutting, the sharp part of it being on both sides longitudinally, as you see; so that the lenticular part is resting upon the meninges while the cutting surface is on the bone.	0.6070026

of the fingers When the fingers are dislocated, they must be gently extended without force, and this is an easy thing.	On the treatment of dislocation of the toes.
